src="/w/load.php?debug=false&amp;lang=en&amp;modules=html5shiv&amp;only=scripts&amp;skin=vector&amp;sync=1"></script><![endif]-->\n</head>\n<body class="mediawiki ltr sitedir-ltr mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject page-Scientific_Revolution rootpage-Scientific_Revolution skin-vector action-view">\t\t<div id="mw-page-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Scientific Revolution</h1>\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about a period in the history of science. For the process of scientific progress via revolutions, proposed by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kuhn" title="Thomas Kuhn">Thomas Kuhn</a>, see <a href="/wiki/Paradigm_shift" title="Paradigm shift">Paradigm shift</a>.</div>\n<p>\nThe <b>Scientific Revolution</b> was a series of events that marked the emergence of  <a href="/wiki/Modern_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern science">modern science</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>, when developments in <a href="/wiki/History_of_mathematics#Mathematics_during_the_Scientific_Revolution" title="History of mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_physics#Scientific_Revolution" title="History of physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_astronomy#Renaissance_Period" title="History of astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_biology#Renaissance_and_early_modern_developments" title="History of biology">biology</a> (including <a href="/wiki/History_of_anatomy" title="History of anatomy">human anatomy</a>) and <a href="/wiki/History_of_chemistry#17th_and_18th_centuries:_Early_chemistry" title="History of chemistry">chemistry</a> transformed the views of society about nature.<sup id="cite_ref-Galileo_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galileo-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moody_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moody-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clagett_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clagett-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011_p342_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011_p342-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Grant_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Scientific Revolution took place in Europe towards the end of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> period and continued through the late 18th century, influencing the intellectual social movement known as <a href="/wiki/The_Enlightenment" class="mw-redirect" title="The Enlightenment">the Enlightenment</a>. While its dates are debated, the publication in 1543 of <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> (<i>On the Revolutions of the Heavenly Spheres</i>) is often cited as marking the beginning of the Scientific Revolution.\n</p><p>The concept of a scientific revolution taking place over an extended period emerged in the eighteenth century in the work of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Sylvain_Bailly" title="Jean Sylvain Bailly">Jean Sylvain Bailly</a>, who saw a two-stage process of sweeping away the old and establishing the new.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The beginning of the Scientific Revolution, the <i>Scientific Renaissance</i>, was focused on the recovery of the knowledge of the ancients; this is generally considered to have ended in 1632 with publication of <a href="/wiki/Galileo" class="mw-redirect" title="Galileo">Galileo</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The completion of the Scientific Revolution is attributed to the "grand synthesis" of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>\'s 1687 <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i>.  The work formulated the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton&#39;s laws of motion">laws of motion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Universal_gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal gravitation">universal gravitation</a> thereby completing the synthesis of a new cosmology.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the 18th century, the Scientific Revolution had given way to the "<a href="/wiki/Romanticism_in_science" title="Romanticism in science">Age of Reflection</a>."\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Introduction"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span method</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Empiricism"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Empiricism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 class="tocnumber">3.5</span> <span class="toctext">The mechanical philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Institutionalization"><span class="tocnumber">3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Institutionalization</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#New_ideas"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">New ideas</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Astronomy"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Astronomy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Biology_and_Medicine"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Biology and Medicine</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Chemistry"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Chemistry</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Physical"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Physical</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#New_mechanical_devices"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">New mechanical devices</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Calculating_devices"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Calculating devices</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Industrial_machines"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Industrial machines</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Telescopes"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Telescopes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Other_devices"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Other devices</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Materials,_construction,_and_aesthetics"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Materials, construction, and aesthetics</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Scientific_developments"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Scientific developments</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Criticism"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Criticism</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Introduction">Introduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Introduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Great advances in science have been termed "revolutions" since the 18th century. In 1747, <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Clairaut" title="Alexis Clairaut">Clairaut</a> wrote that "<a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a> was said in his own lifetime to have created a revolution".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The word was also used in the preface to <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Lavoisier" title="Antoine Lavoisier">Lavoisier</a>\'s 1789 work announcing the discovery of oxygen. "Few revolutions in science have immediately excited so much general notice as the introduction of the theory of oxygen ... Lavoisier saw his theory accepted by all the most eminent men of his time, and established over a great part of Europe within a few years from its first promulgation."<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/William_Whewell" title="William Whewell">William Whewell</a> described the revolution in <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> itself\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>\xe2\x80\x94that had taken place in the 15th\xe2\x80\x9316th century. "Among the most conspicuous of the revolutions which opinions on this subject have undergone, is the transition from an implicit trust in the internal powers of man\'s mind to a professed dependence upon external observation; and from an unbounded reverence for the wisdom of the past, to a fervid expectation of change and improvement."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> This gave rise to the common view of the Scientific Revolution today:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>A new view of nature emerged, replacing the Greek view that had dominated science for almost 2,000 years. Science became an autonomous discipline, distinct from both philosophy and technology and came to be regarded as having utilitarian goals.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Galilei_by_Ottavio_Leoni_Marucelliana_(cropped).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Galileo_Galilei_by_Ottavio_Leoni_Marucelliana_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Galileo_Galilei_by_Ottavio_Leoni_Marucelliana_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="170" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Galileo_Galilei_by_Ottavio_Leoni_Marucelliana_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Galileo_Galilei_by_Ottavio_Leoni_Marucelliana_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Galileo_Galilei_by_Ottavio_Leoni_Marucelliana_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Galileo_Galilei_by_Ottavio_Leoni_Marucelliana_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="483" data-file-height="552" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Galilei_by_Ottavio_Leoni_Marucelliana_(cropped).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ottavio_Leoni" title="Ottavio Leoni">Leoni</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Scientific Revolution is traditionally assumed to start with the <a href="/wiki/Copernican_Revolution" title="Copernican Revolution">Copernican Revolution</a> (initiated in 1543) and to be complete in the "grand synthesis" of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>\'s 1687 <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i>. Much of the change of attitude came from <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> whose "confident and emphatic announcement" in the modern progress of science inspired the creation of scientific societies such as the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> who championed <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Copernicus</a> and developed the science of motion.\n</p><p>In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Koyr%C3%A9" title="Alexandre Koyr\xc3\xa9">Alexandre Koyr\xc3\xa9</a> introduced the term "scientific revolution", centering his analysis on Galileo. The term was popularized by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Butterfield" title="Herbert Butterfield">Butterfield</a> in his <i>Origins of Modern Science</i>. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kuhn" title="Thomas Kuhn">Thomas Kuhn</a>\'s 1962 work <i><a href="/wiki/The_Structure_of_Scientific_Revolutions" title="The Structure of Scientific Revolutions">The Structure of Scientific Revolutions</a></i> emphasized that different theoretical frameworks\xe2\x80\x94such as <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Relativity_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Relativity theory">relativity theory</a> and Newton\'s theory of gravity, which it replaced\xe2\x80\x94cannot be directly compared.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Significance">Significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The period saw a fundamental transformation in scientific ideas across mathematics, physics, astronomy, and biology in institutions supporting scientific investigation and in the more widely held picture of the universe. The Scientific Revolution led to the establishment of several modern sciences. In 1984, Joseph Ben-David wrote:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Rapid accumulation of knowledge, which has characterized the development of science since the 17th century, had never occurred before that time. The new kind of scientific activity emerged only in a few countries of Western Europe, and it was restricted to that small area for about two hundred years. (Since the 19th century, scientific knowledge has been assimilated by the rest of the world).<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Many contemporary writers and modern historians claim that there was a revolutionary change in world view. In 1611 the English poet, <a href="/wiki/John_Donne" title="John Donne">John Donne</a>, wrote:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>[The] new Philosophy calls all in doubt,<br />\n</p><p>The Element of fire is quite put out;<br />\nThe Sun is lost, and th\'earth, and no man\'s wit<br />\nCan well direct him where to look for it.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Mid-20th-century historian <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Butterfield" title="Herbert Butterfield">Herbert Butterfield</a> was less disconcerted, but nevertheless saw the change as fundamental:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Since that revolution turned the authority in English not only of the Middle Ages but of the ancient world\xe2\x80\x94since it started not only in the eclipse of scholastic philosophy but in the destruction of Aristotelian physics\xe2\x80\x94it outshines everything since the rise of Christianity and reduces the Renaissance and Reformation to the rank of mere episodes, mere internal displacements within the system of medieval Christendom.... [It] looms so large as the real origin both of the modern world and of the modern mentality that our customary periodization of European history has become an anachronism and an encumbrance.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The history professor <a href="/wiki/Peter_Harrison_(historian)" title="Peter Harrison (historian)">Peter Harrison</a> attributes Christianity to having contributed to the rise of the Scientific Revolution:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> historians of science have long known that religious factors played a significantly positive role in the emergence and persistence of modern science in the West. Not only were many of the key figures in the rise of science individuals with sincere religious commitments, but the new approaches to nature that they pioneered were underpinned in various ways by religious assumptions. ... Yet, many of the leading figures in the scientific revolution imagined themselves to be champions of a science that was more compatible with Christianity than the medieval ideas about the natural world that they replaced.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_medieval_background">Ancient and medieval background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ancient and medieval background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PeuerbachSuperioribus2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/PeuerbachSuperioribus2.png/220px-PeuerbachSuperioribus2.png" width="220" height="295" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/PeuerbachSuperioribus2.png/330px-PeuerbachSuperioribus2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/PeuerbachSuperioribus2.png/440px-PeuerbachSuperioribus2.png 2x" data-file-width="1141" data-file-height="1529" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PeuerbachSuperioribus2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemaic system">Ptolemaic model</a> of the spheres for <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>. <a href="/wiki/Georg_von_Peuerbach" title="Georg von Peuerbach">Georg von Peuerbach</a>, <i>Theoricae novae planetarum</i>, 1474.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_Physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Aristotelian Physics">Aristotelian Physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Science in the Middle Ages">Science in the Middle Ages</a></div>\n<p>The Scientific Revolution was built upon the foundation of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek</a> learning and <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Science in the Middle Ages">science in the Middle Ages</a>, as it had been elaborated and further developed by <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_science" title="Byzantine science">Roman/Byzantine science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic science">medieval Islamic science</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Grant_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grant-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Some scholars have noted a direct tie between "particular aspects of traditional Christianity" and the rise of science.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>  \nThe "<a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian tradition</a>" was still an important intellectual framework in the 17th century, although by that time <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophers" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural philosophers">natural philosophers</a> had moved away from much of it.<sup id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011_p342_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011_p342-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Key scientific ideas dating back to <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> had changed drastically over the years, and in many cases been discredited.<sup id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011_p342_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011_p342-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The ideas that remained, which were transformed fundamentally during the Scientific Revolution, include:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>\'s cosmetics that placed the Earth at the center of a spherical hierarchic <a href="/wiki/Cosmos" title="Cosmos">cosmos</a>. The terrestrial and celestial regions were made up of different elements which had different kinds of <i>natural movement</i>.\n<ul><li>The terrestrial region, according to Aristotle, consisted of concentric spheres of the four <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">elements</a>\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/Earth_(classical_element)" title="Earth (classical element)">earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_(classical_element)" title="Air (classical element)">air</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">fire</a>. All bodies naturally moved in straight lines until they reached the sphere appropriate to their elemental composition\xe2\x80\x94their <i>natural place</i>. All other terrestrial motions were non-natural, or <i>violent</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>The celestial region was made up of the fifth element, <a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">aether</a>, which was unchanging and moved naturally with <a href="/wiki/Uniform_circular_motion" class="mw-redirect" title="Uniform circular motion">uniform circular motion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> In the Aristotelian tradition, astronomical theories sought to explain the observed irregular motion of celestial objects through the combined effects of multiple uniform circular motions.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Ptolemaic system">Ptolemaic model of planetary motion</a>: based on the geometrical model of <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxus of Cnidus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Almagest</a></i>, demonstrated that calculations could compute the exact positions of the Sun, Moon, stars, and planets in the future and in the past, and showed how these computational models were derived from astronomical observations. As such they formed the model for later astronomical developments. The physical basis for Ptolemaic models invoked layers of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres" title="Celestial spheres">spherical shells</a>, though the most complex models were inconsistent with this physical explanation.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>It is important to note that ancient precedent existed for alternative theories and developments which prefigured later discoveries in the area of physics and mechanics; but in light of the limited number of works to survive translation in a period when many books were lost to warfare, such developments remained obscure for centuries and are traditionally held to have had little effect on the re-discovery of such phenomena; whereas the invention of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> made the wide dissemination of such incremental advances of knowledge commonplace. Meanwhile, however, significant progress in geometry, mathematics, and astronomy was made in medieval times.\n</p><p>It is also true that many of the important figures of the Scientific Revolution shared in the general <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> respect for ancient learning and cited ancient pedigrees for their innovations. <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> (1473\xe2\x80\x931543),<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> (1564\xe2\x80\x931642),<sup id="cite_ref-Galileo_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Galileo-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moody_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moody-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clagett_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clagett-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a> (1571\xe2\x80\x931630)<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton</a> (1642\xe2\x80\x931727),<sup id="cite_ref-rattansi_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rattansi-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> all traced different ancient and medieval ancestries for the <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric system</a>. In the Axioms Scholium of his <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a>,</i> Newton said its axiomatic <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton&#39;s laws of motion">three laws of motion</a> were already accepted by mathematicians such as <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Huygens</a> (1629\xe2\x80\x931695), Wallace, Wren and others.  While preparing a revised edition of his <i>Principia</i>, Newton attributed his <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">law of gravity</a> and his <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion#Newton&#39;s_first_law:_law_of_inertia" title="Newton&#39;s laws of motion">first law of motion</a> to a range of historical figures.<sup id="cite_ref-rattansi_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rattansi-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Unpublished_Scientific_Papers_of_Isaac_Newton_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unpublished_Scientific_Papers_of_Isaac_Newton-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Despite these qualifications, the standard theory of the history of the Scientific Revolution claims that the 17th century was a period of revolutionary scientific changes. Not only were there revolutionary theoretical and experimental developments, but that even more importantly, the way in which scientists worked was radically changed. For instance, although intimations of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a> are suggested sporadically in ancient discussion of motion,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\nthe salient point is that Newton\'s theory differed from ancient understandings in key ways, such as an external force being a requirement for violent motion in Aristotle\'s theory.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_method">Scientific method</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Scientific method">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Under the scientific method as conceived in the 17th century, natural and artificial circumstances were set aside as a research tradition of systematic experimentation was slowly accepted by the scientific community. The philosophy of using an <a href="/wiki/Induction_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Induction (philosophy)">inductive</a> approach to obtain knowledge \xe2\x80\x94 to abandon assumption and to attempt to observe with an open mind \xe2\x80\x94 was in contrast with the earlier, Aristotelian approach of <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deduction</a>, by which analysis of known facts produced further understanding. In practice, many scientists and philosophers believed that a healthy mix of both was needed \xe2\x80\x94 the willingness to question assumptions, yet also to interpret observations assumed to have some degree of validity.\n</p><p>By the end of the Scientific Revolution the qualitative world of book-reading philosophers had been changed into a mechanical, mathematical world to be known through experimental research. Though it is certainly not true that <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Newtonian science">Newtonian science</a> was like modern science in all respects, it conceptually resembled ours in many ways. Many of the hallmarks of <a href="/wiki/Modern_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern science">modern science</a>, especially with regard to its institutionalization and professionalization, did not become standard until the mid-19th century.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Empiricism">Empiricism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Empiricism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The Aristotelian scientific tradition\'s primary mode of interacting with the world was through observation and searching for "natural" circumstances through reasoning. Coupled with this approach was the belief that rare events which seemed to contradict theoretical models were aberrations, telling nothing about nature as it "naturally" was. During the Scientific Revolution, changing perceptions about the role of the scientist in respect to nature, the value of evidence, experimental or observed, led towards a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific methodology</a> in which <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a> played a large, but not absolute, role.\n</p><p>By the start of the Scientific Revolution, empiricism had already become an important component of science and natural philosophy. <a href="/wiki/European_science_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="European science in the Middle Ages">Prior thinkers</a>, including the early-14th-century <a href="/wiki/Nominalism" title="Nominalism">nominalist</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>, had begun the intellectual movement toward empiricism.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term British empiricism came into use to describe philosophical differences perceived between two of its founders <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, described as empiricist, and <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes">Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes</a>, who was described as a rationalist. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">George Berkeley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> were the philosophy\'s primary exponents, who developed a sophisticated empirical tradition as the basis of human knowledge.\n</p><p>An influential formulation of empiricism was <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_Concerning_Human_Understanding" title="An Essay Concerning Human Understanding">An Essay Concerning Human Understanding</a></i> (1689), in which he maintained that the only true knowledge that could be accessible to the human mind was that which was based on experience. He wrote that the human mind was created as  a <i><a href="/wiki/Tabula_rasa" title="Tabula rasa">tabula rasa</a></i>, a "blank tablet," upon which sensory impressions were recorded and built up knowledge through a process of reflection.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baconian_science">Baconian science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Baconian science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Somer_Francis_Bacon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Somer_Francis_Bacon.jpg/220px-Somer_Francis_Bacon.jpg" width="220" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Somer_Francis_Bacon.jpg/330px-Somer_Francis_Bacon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Somer_Francis_Bacon.jpg/440px-Somer_Francis_Bacon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1629" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Somer_Francis_Bacon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> was a pivotal figure in establishing the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a> of investigation. Portrait by <a href="/wiki/Frans_Pourbus_the_Younger" title="Frans Pourbus the Younger">Frans Pourbus the Younger</a> (1617).</div></div></div>\n<p>The philosophical underpinnings of the Scientific Revolution were laid out by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, who has been called the father of <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> His works established and popularised <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive</a> methodologies for scientific inquiry, often called the <i><a href="/wiki/Baconian_method" title="Baconian method">Baconian method</a></i>, or simply the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>. His demand for a planned procedure of investigating all things natural marked a new turn in the rhetorical and theoretical framework for science, much of which still surrounds conceptions of proper <a href="/wiki/Methodology" title="Methodology">methodology</a> today.\n</p><p>Bacon proposed a great reformation of all process of knowledge for the advancement of learning divine and human, which he called <i>Instauratio Magna</i> (The Great Instauration). For Bacon, this reformation would lead to a great advancement in science and a progeny of new inventions that would relieve mankind\'s miseries and needs. His <i><a href="/wiki/Novum_Organum" title="Novum Organum">Novum Organum</a></i> was published in 1620. He argued that man is "the minister and interpreter of nature", that "knowledge and human power are synonymous", that "effects are produced by the means of instruments and helps", and that "man while operating can only apply or withdraw natural bodies; nature internally performs the rest", and later that "nature can only be commanded by obeying her".<sup id="cite_ref-Novum_Organum_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novum_Organum-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Here is an abstract of the philosophy of this work, that by the knowledge of nature and the using of instruments, man can govern or direct the natural work of nature to produce definite results.  Therefore, that man, by seeking knowledge of nature, can reach power over it \xe2\x80\x93 and thus reestablish the "Empire of Man over creation", which had been lost by the Fall together with man\'s original purity. In this way, he believed, would mankind be raised above conditions of helplessness, poverty and misery, while coming into a condition of peace, prosperity and security.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For this purpose of obtaining knowledge of and power over nature, Bacon outlined in this work a new system of logic he believed to be superior to the old ways of <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogism</a>, developing his scientific method, consisting of procedures for isolating the formal cause of a phenomenon (heat, for example) through eliminative induction. For him, the philosopher should proceed through <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a> from <a href="/wiki/Fact" title="Fact">fact</a> to <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axiom</a> to <a href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical law</a>. Before beginning this induction, though, the enquirer must free his or her mind from certain false notions or tendencies which distort the truth. In particular, he found that philosophy was too preoccupied with words, particularly discourse and debate, rather than actually observing the material world: "For while men believe their reason governs words, in fact, words turn back and reflect their power upon the understanding, and so render philosophy and science sophistical and inactive."<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bacon considered that it is of greatest importance to science not to keep doing intellectual discussions or seeking merely contemplative aims, but that it should work for the bettering of mankind\'s life by bringing forth new inventions, having even stated that "inventions are also, as it were, new creations and imitations of divine works".<sup id="cite_ref-Novum_Organum_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novum_Organum-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (January 2014)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> He explored the far-reaching and world-changing character of inventions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_experimentation">Scientific experimentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Scientific experimentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Bacon first described the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_experimentation" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific experimentation">experimental method</a>.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> There remains simple experience; which, if taken as it comes, is called accident, if sought for, experiment. The true method of experience first lights the candle [hypothesis], and then by means of the candle shows the way [arranges and delimits the experiment]; commencing as it does with experience duly ordered and digested, not bungling or erratic, and from it deducing axioms [theories], and from established axioms again new experiments. </p><div class="templatequotecite">\xe2\x80\x94&#8201;<cite>Francis Bacon. <i>Novum Organum.</i> 1620.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p><a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a> was an early advocate of this method. He passionately rejected both the prevailing <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Aristotelian philosophy">Aristotelian philosophy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholastic</a> method of university teaching.  His book <i><a href="/wiki/De_Magnete" title="De Magnete">De Magnete</a></i> was written in 1600, and he is regarded by some as the father of <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In this work, he describes many of his experiments with his model Earth called the <a href="/wiki/Terrella" title="Terrella">terrella</a>. From these experiments, he concluded that the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth&#39;s magnetic field">Earth</a> was itself <a href="/wiki/Magnetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Magnetic">magnetic</a> and that this was the reason <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compasses</a> point north.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_De_Magnete_Illo044.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gilbert_De_Magnete_Illo044.jpg/220px-Gilbert_De_Magnete_Illo044.jpg" width="220" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gilbert_De_Magnete_Illo044.jpg/330px-Gilbert_De_Magnete_Illo044.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Gilbert_De_Magnete_Illo044.jpg/440px-Gilbert_De_Magnete_Illo044.jpg 2x" data-file-width="731" data-file-height="736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_De_Magnete_Illo044.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram from <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/De_Magnete" title="De Magnete">De Magnete</a></i>, a pioneering work of experimental science</div></div></div>\n<p><i>De Magnete</i> was influential not only because of the inherent interest of its subject matter, but also for the rigorous way in which Gilbert described his experiments and his rejection of ancient theories of magnetism.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Thomson_(chemist)" title="Thomas Thomson (chemist)">Thomas Thomson</a>, "Gilbert[\'s]... book on magnetism published in 1600, is one of the finest examples of inductive philosophy that has ever been presented to the world. It is the more remarkable, because it preceded the <i>Novum Organum</i> of Bacon, in which the inductive method of philosophizing was first explained."<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> has been called the "father of modern <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">observational astronomy</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> the "father of modern <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Einstein_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> the "father of science",<sup id="cite_ref-Einstein_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> and "the Father of Modern Science".<sup id="cite_ref-finocchiaro2007_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-finocchiaro2007-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> His original contributions to the science of motion were made through an innovative combination of experiment and mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Galileo_manuscript.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Galileo_manuscript.png/220px-Galileo_manuscript.png" width="220" height="314" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Galileo_manuscript.png/330px-Galileo_manuscript.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Galileo_manuscript.png/440px-Galileo_manuscript.png 2x" data-file-width="569" data-file-height="813" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Galileo_manuscript.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>On this page <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> first noted the <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. Galileo revolutionized the study of the natural world with his rigorous experimental method.</div></div></div>\n<p>Galileo was one of the first modern thinkers to clearly state that the <a href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">laws of nature</a> are mathematical. In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></i> he wrote "Philosophy is written in this grand book, the universe&#160;... It is written in the language of mathematics, and its characters are triangles, circles, and other geometric figures;...."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> His mathematical analyses are a further development of a tradition employed by late scholastic natural philosophers, which Galileo learned when he studied philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> He ignored Aristotelianism. In broader terms, his work marked another step towards the eventual separation of science from both <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> and religion; a major development in human thought. He was often willing to change his views in accordance with observation. In order to perform his experiments, Galileo had to set up standards of length and time, so that measurements made on different days and in different laboratories could be compared in a reproducible fashion. This provided a reliable foundation on which to confirm mathematical laws using <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a>.\n</p><p>Galileo showed an appreciation for the relationship between mathematics, theoretical physics, and experimental physics. He understood the <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>, both in terms of <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic sections</a> and in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Ordinate" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordinate">ordinate</a> (y) varying as the square of the <a href="/wiki/Abscissa" class="mw-redirect" title="Abscissa">abscissa</a> (x). Galilei further asserted that the parabola was the theoretically ideal <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectory</a> of a uniformly accelerated projectile in the absence of <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> and other disturbances. He conceded that there are limits to the validity of this theory, noting on theoretical grounds that a projectile trajectory of a size comparable to that of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> could not possibly be a parabola,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> but he nevertheless maintained that for distances up to the range of the artillery of his day, the deviation of a projectile\'s trajectory from a parabola would be only very slight.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematization">Mathematization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mathematization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Scientific knowledge, according to the Aristotelians, was concerned with establishing true and necessary causes of things.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> To the extent that medieval natural philosophers used mathematical problems, they limited social studies to theoretical analyses of local speed and other aspects of life.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The actual measurement of a physical quantity, and the comparison of that measurement to a value computed on the basis of theory, was largely limited to the mathematical disciplines of <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a> in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-astronomies_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-astronomies-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 16th and 17th centuries, European scientists began increasingly applying quantitative measurements to the measurement of physical phenomena on the Earth. Galileo maintained strongly that mathematics provided a kind of necessary certainty that could be compared to God\'s: "...with regard to those few [mathematical <a href="/wiki/Theorem" title="Theorem">propositions</a>] which the human intellect does understand, I believe its knowledge equals the Divine in objective certainty..."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> anticipates the concept of a systematic mathematical interpretation of the world in his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">Il Saggiatore</a></i>:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Philosophy [i.e., physics] is written in this grand book\xe2\x80\x94I mean the universe\xe2\x80\x94which stands continually open to our gaze, but it cannot be understood unless one first learns to comprehend the language and interpret the characters in which it is written. It is written in the language of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, and its characters are triangles, circles, and other geometrical figures, without which it is humanly impossible to understand a single word of it; without these, one is wandering around in a dark labyrinth.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_mechanical_philosophy">The mechanical philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The mechanical philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/220px-Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg" width="220" height="267" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/330px-Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg/440px-Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller%2C_Bt.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Isaac_Newton_by_Sir_Godfrey_Kneller,_Bt.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> in a 1702 portrait by <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Kneller" title="Godfrey Kneller">Godfrey Kneller</a></div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy" title="Mechanical philosophy">Mechanical philosophy</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> recognized four kinds of causes, and where applicable, the most important of them is the "final cause". The final cause was the aim, goal, or purpose of some natural process or man-made thing. Until the Scientific Revolution, it was very natural to see such aims, such as a child\'s growth, for example, leading to a mature adult. Intelligence was assumed only in the purpose of man-made artifacts; it was not attributed to other animals or to nature.\n</p><p>In "<a href="/wiki/Mechanical_philosophy" title="Mechanical philosophy">mechanical philosophy</a>" no field or action at a distance is permitted, particles or corpuscles of matter are fundamentally inert. Motion is caused by direct physical collision. Where natural substances had previously been understood organically, the mechanical philosophers viewed them as machines.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> As a result, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>\'s theory seemed like some kind of throwback to "spooky action at a distance". According to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kuhn" title="Thomas Kuhn">Thomas Kuhn</a>, Newton and <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes">Descartes</a> held the <a href="/wiki/Teleology" title="Teleology">teleological principle</a> that God conserved the amount of motion in the universe:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Gravity, interpreted as an innate attraction between every pair of particles of matter, was an occult quality in the same sense as the scholastics\' "tendency to fall" had been.... By the mid eighteenth century that interpretation had been almost universally accepted, and the result was a genuine reversion (which is not the same as a retrogression) to a scholastic standard. Innate attractions and repulsions joined size, shape, position and motion as physically irreducible primary properties of matter.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Newton had also specifically attributed the inherent power of inertia to matter, against the mechanist thesis that matter has no inherent powers. But whereas Newton vehemently denied gravity was an inherent power of matter, his collaborator <a href="/wiki/Roger_Cotes" title="Roger Cotes">Roger Cotes</a> made gravity also an inherent power of matter, as set out in his famous preface to the <i>Principia\'s</i> 1713 second edition which he edited, and contradicted Newton himself. And it was Cotes\'s interpretation of gravity rather than Newton\'s that came to be accepted.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Institutionalization">Institutionalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Institutionalization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gresham_College_from_Record_of_RS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Gresham_College_from_Record_of_RS.jpg/220px-Gresham_College_from_Record_of_RS.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Gresham_College_from_Record_of_RS.jpg/330px-Gresham_College_from_Record_of_RS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Gresham_College_from_Record_of_RS.jpg/440px-Gresham_College_from_Record_of_RS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="584" data-file-height="387" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gresham_College_from_Record_of_RS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> had its origins in <a href="/wiki/Gresham_College" title="Gresham College">Gresham College</a>, and was the first scientific society in the world.</div></div></div>\n<p>The first moves towards the institutionalization of scientific investigation and dissemination took the form of the establishment of societies, where new discoveries were aired, discussed and published. The first scientific society to be established was the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> of London. This grew out of an earlier group, centred around <a href="/wiki/Gresham_College_and_the_formation_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Gresham College and the formation of the Royal Society">Gresham College</a> in the 1640s and 1650s. According to a history of the College:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>The scientific network which centred on Gresham College played a crucial part in the meetings which led to the formation of the Royal Society.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>These physicians and <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosopher" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural philosopher">natural philosophers</a> were influenced by the "<a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">new science</a>", as promoted by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/New_Atlantis" title="New Atlantis">New Atlantis</a></i>, from approximately 1645 onwards. A group known as <i>The Philosophical Society of Oxford</i> was run under a set of rules still retained by the <a href="/wiki/Bodleian_Library" title="Bodleian Library">Bodleian Library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 28 November 1660, the <a href="/wiki/Gresham_College_and_the_formation_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Gresham College and the formation of the Royal Society">1660 committee of 12</a> announced the formation of a "College for the Promoting of Physico-Mathematical Experimental Learning", which would meet weekly to discuss science and run experiments. At the second meeting, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Moray" title="Robert Moray">Robert Moray</a> announced that the <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">King</a> approved of the gatherings, and a <a href="/wiki/Royal_charter" title="Royal charter">Royal charter</a> was signed on 15 July 1662 creating the "Royal Society of London", with <a href="/wiki/William_Brouncker,_2nd_Viscount_Brouncker" title="William Brouncker, 2nd Viscount Brouncker">Lord Brouncker</a> serving as the first President. A second Royal Charter was signed on 23 April 1663, with the King noted as the Founder and with the name of "the Royal Society of London for the Improvement of Natural Knowledge"; Robert Hooke was appointed as Curator of Experiments in November. This initial royal favour has continued, and since then every monarch has been the patron of the Society.<sup id="cite_ref-pw_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pw-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences_1698.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences_1698.jpg/220px-Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences_1698.jpg" width="220" height="137" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences_1698.jpg/330px-Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences_1698.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences_1698.jpg/440px-Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences_1698.jpg 2x" data-file-width="836" data-file-height="521" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences_1698.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The French <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy of Sciences">Academy of Sciences</a> was established in 1666.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Society\'s first Secretary was <a href="/wiki/Henry_Oldenburg" title="Henry Oldenburg">Henry Oldenburg</a>. Its early meetings included experiments performed first by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> and then by <a href="/wiki/Denis_Papin" title="Denis Papin">Denis Papin</a>, who was appointed in 1684. These experiments varied in their subject area, and were both important in some cases and trivial in others.<sup id="cite_ref-hen1_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hen1-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The society began publication of <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophical_Transactions_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Philosophical Transactions of the Royal Society">Philosophical Transactions</a></i> from 1665, the oldest and longest-running scientific journal in the world, which established the important principles of <a href="/wiki/Scientific_priority" title="Scientific priority">scientific priority</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peer_review" title="Peer review">peer review</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The French established the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy of Sciences">Academy of Sciences</a> in 1666. In contrast to the private origins of its British counterpart, the Academy was founded as a government body by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Colbert" title="Jean-Baptiste Colbert">Jean-Baptiste Colbert</a>. Its rules were set down in 1699 by King <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV" class="mw-redirect" title="Louis XIV">Louis XIV</a>, when it received the name of \'Royal Academy of Sciences\' and was installed in the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> in Paris.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="New_ideas">New ideas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: New ideas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>As the Scientific Revolution was not marked by any single change, the following new ideas contributed to what is called the Scientific Revolution. Many of them were revolutions in their own fields.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Astronomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<dl><dt>Heliocentrism</dt></dl>\n<p>For almost five <a href="/wiki/Millennia" class="mw-redirect" title="Millennia">millennia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model" title="Geocentric model">geocentric model</a> of the Earth as the center of the universe had been accepted by all but a few astronomers. In Aristotle\'s cosmology, Earth\'s central location was perhaps less significant than its identification as a realm of imperfection, inconstancy, irregularity and change, as opposed to the "heavens" (Moon, Sun, planets, stars), which were regarded as perfect, permanent, unchangeable, and in religious thought, the realm of heavenly beings. The Earth was even composed of different material, the four elements "earth", "water", "fire", and "air", while sufficiently far above its surface (roughly the Moon\'s orbit), the heavens were composed of different substance called "aether".<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentric model</a> that replaced it involved not only the radical displacement of the earth to an orbit around the sun, but its sharing a placement with the other planets implied a universe of heavenly components made from the same changeable substances as the Earth. Heavenly motions no longer needed to be governed by a theoretical perfection, confined to circular orbits.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JKepler.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/JKepler.jpg/220px-JKepler.jpg" width="220" height="281" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/JKepler.jpg/330px-JKepler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/JKepler.jpg/440px-JKepler.jpg 2x" data-file-width="470" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JKepler.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Copernicus\' 1543 work on the heliocentric model of the solar system tried to demonstrate that the sun was the center of the universe. Few were bothered by this suggestion, and the pope and several archbishops were interested enough by it to want more detail.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> His model was later used to create the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">calendar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIII" title="Pope Gregory XIII">Pope Gregory XIII</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> However, the idea that the earth moved around the sun was doubted by most of Copernicus\' contemporaries. It contradicted not only empirical observation, due to the absence of an observable <a href="/wiki/Stellar_parallax" title="Stellar parallax">stellar parallax</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> but more significantly at the time, the authority of Aristotle.\n</p><p>The discoveries of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galileo" class="mw-redirect" title="Galileo">Galileo</a> gave the theory credibility. Kepler was an astronomer who, using the accurate observations of <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a>, proposed that the planets move around the sun not in circular orbits, but in elliptical ones. Together with his other <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler&#39;s laws of planetary motion">laws of planetary motion</a>, this allowed him to create a model of the solar system that was an improvement over Copernicus\' original system. Galileo\'s main contributions to the acceptance of the heliocentric system were his mechanics, the observations he made with his telescope, as well as his detailed presentation of the case for the system. Using an early theory of <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a>, Galileo could explain why rocks dropped from a tower fall straight down even if the earth rotates. His observations of the moons of Jupiter, the phases of Venus, the spots on the sun, and mountains on the moon all helped to discredit the Aristotelian philosophy and the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemaic</a> theory of the solar system. Through their combined discoveries, the heliocentric system gained support, and at the end of the 17th century it was generally accepted by astronomers.\n</p><p>This work culminated in the work of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>. Newton\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i> formulated the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton&#39;s laws of motion">laws of motion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">universal gravitation</a>, which dominated scientists\' view of the physical universe for the next three centuries. By deriving Kepler\'s laws of planetary motion from his mathematical description of <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, and then using the same principles to account for the trajectories of <a href="/wiki/Comets" class="mw-redirect" title="Comets">comets</a>, the tides, the precession of the equinoxes, and other phenomena, Newton removed the last doubts about the validity of the heliocentric model of the cosmos. This work also demonstrated that the motion of objects on Earth and of celestial bodies could be described by the same principles. His prediction that the Earth should be shaped as an oblate spheroid was later vindicated by other scientists. His <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton&#39;s laws of motion">laws of motion</a> were to be the solid foundation of mechanics; his <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">law of universal gravitation</a> combined terrestrial and celestial mechanics into one great system that seemed to be able to describe the whole world in mathematical <a href="/wiki/Formula" title="Formula">formulae</a>.\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Gravitation</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NewtonsPrincipia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/NewtonsPrincipia.jpg/220px-NewtonsPrincipia.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/NewtonsPrincipia.jpg/330px-NewtonsPrincipia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/NewtonsPrincipia.jpg/440px-NewtonsPrincipia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1266" data-file-height="842" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NewtonsPrincipia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i>, developed the first set of unified scientific laws.</div></div></div>\n<p>As well as proving the heliocentric model, Newton also developed the <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" class="mw-redirect" title="Gravitation">theory of gravitation</a>. In 1679, Newton began to consider gravitation and its effect on the orbits of <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> with reference to <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Kepler&#39;s laws">Kepler\'s laws</a> of planetary motion. This followed stimulation by a brief exchange of letters in 1679\xe2\x80\x9380 with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a>, who had been appointed to manage the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>\'s correspondence, and who opened a correspondence intended to elicit contributions from Newton to Royal Society transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Newton\'s reawakening interest in astronomical matters received further stimulus by the appearance of a comet in the winter of 1680\xe2\x80\x931681, on which he corresponded with <a href="/wiki/John_Flamsteed" title="John Flamsteed">John Flamsteed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> After the exchanges with Hooke, Newton worked out proof that the elliptical form of planetary orbits would result from a centripetal force <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">inversely proportional to the square of the radius vector</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation#History" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">Newton\'s law of universal gravitation \xe2\x80\x93 History</a> and <i>De motu corporum in gyrum</i>). Newton communicated his results to <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a> and to the Royal Society in <i><a href="/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum" title="De motu corporum in gyrum">De motu corporum in gyrum</a></i>, in 1684.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> This tract contained the nucleus that Newton developed and expanded to form the <i>Principia</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Principia</a></i> was published on 5 July 1687 with encouragement and financial help from <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">Edmond Halley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In this work, Newton stated the <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton#Laws_of_motion" title="Isaac Newton">three universal laws of motion</a> that contributed to many advances during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> which soon followed and were not to be improved upon for more than 200 years.  Many of these advancements continue to be the underpinnings of non-relativistic technologies in the modern world. He used the Latin word <i>gravitas</i> (weight) for the effect that would become known as <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a>, and defined the law of <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">universal gravitation</a>.\n</p><p>Newton\'s postulate of an invisible <a href="/wiki/Action_at_a_distance" title="Action at a distance">force able to act over vast distances</a> led to him being criticised for introducing "<a href="/wiki/Occult" title="Occult">occult</a> agencies" into science.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Later, in the second edition of the <i>Principia</i> (1713), Newton firmly rejected such criticisms in a concluding <a href="/wiki/General_Scholium" title="General Scholium">General Scholium</a>, writing that it was enough that the phenomena implied a gravitational attraction, as they did; but they did not so far indicate its cause, and it was both unnecessary and improper to frame hypotheses of things that were not implied by the phenomena. (Here Newton used what became his famous expression "hypotheses non fingo"<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology_and_Medicine">Biology and Medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Biology and Medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<dl><dt>Medical discoveries</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vesalius_Fabrica_p190.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Vesalius_Fabrica_p190.jpg/200px-Vesalius_Fabrica_p190.jpg" width="200" height="351" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Vesalius_Fabrica_p190.jpg/300px-Vesalius_Fabrica_p190.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Vesalius_Fabrica_p190.jpg/400px-Vesalius_Fabrica_p190.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1022" data-file-height="1792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vesalius_Fabrica_p190.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Vesalius</a>\'s intricately detailed drawings of human dissections in <i>Fabrica</i> helped to overturn the medical theories of <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The writings of Greek physician <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a> had dominated European medical thinking for over a millennium. The Flemish scholar <a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Vesalius</a> demonstrated mistakes in the Galen\'s ideas. Vesalius dissected human corpses, whereas Galen dissected animal corpses. Published in 1543, Vesalius\' <i><a href="/wiki/De_humani_corporis_fabrica" title="De humani corporis fabrica">De humani corporis fabrica</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> was a groundbreaking work of <a href="/wiki/Human_anatomy" class="mw-redirect" title="Human anatomy">human anatomy</a>. It emphasized the priority of dissection and what has come to be called the "anatomical" view of the body, seeing human internal functioning as an essentially corporeal structure filled with organs arranged in three-dimensional space.
