This was in stark contrast to many of the anatomical models used previously, which had strong Galenic/Aristotelean elements, as well as elements of <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>.\n</p><p>Besides the first good description of the <a href="/wiki/Sphenoid_bone" title="Sphenoid bone">sphenoid bone</a>, he showed that the <a href="/wiki/Sternum" title="Sternum">sternum</a> consists of three portions and the <a href="/wiki/Sacrum" title="Sacrum">sacrum</a> of five or six; and described accurately the <a href="/wiki/Vestibule_of_the_ear" title="Vestibule of the ear">vestibule</a> in the interior of the temporal bone. He not only verified the observation of Etienne on the valves of the hepatic veins, but he described the <a href="/wiki/Azygos_vein" title="Azygos vein">vena azygos</a>, and discovered the canal which passes in the fetus between the umbilical vein and the vena cava, since named <a href="/wiki/Ductus_venosus" title="Ductus venosus">ductus venosus</a>. He described the <a href="/wiki/Greater_omentum" title="Greater omentum">omentum</a>, and its connections with the stomach, the <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Colon_(anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Colon (anatomy)">colon</a>; gave the first correct views of the structure of the <a href="/wiki/Pylorus" title="Pylorus">pylorus</a>; observed the small size of the caecal appendix in man; gave the first good account of the <a href="/wiki/Mediastinum" title="Mediastinum">mediastinum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pleura" class="mw-redirect" title="Pleura">pleura</a> and the fullest description of the anatomy of the brain yet advanced. He did not understand the inferior recesses; and his account of the nerves is confused by regarding the optic as the first pair, the third as the fifth and the fifth as the seventh.\n</p><p>Further groundbreaking work was carried out by <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a>, who published <i>De Motu Cordis</i> in 1628. Harvey made a detailed analysis of the overall structure of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, going on to an analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Arteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Arteries">arteries</a>, showing how their pulsation depends upon the contraction of the <a href="/wiki/Left_ventricle" class="mw-redirect" title="Left ventricle">left ventricle</a>, while the contraction of the <a href="/wiki/Right_ventricle" class="mw-redirect" title="Right ventricle">right ventricle</a> propels its charge of blood into the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_artery" title="Pulmonary artery">pulmonary artery</a>. He noticed that the two <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">ventricles</a> move together almost simultaneously and not independently like had been thought previously by his predecessors.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/220px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/330px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg/440px-William_Harvey_%281578-1657%29_Venenbild.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1293" data-file-height="901" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Harvey_(1578-1657)_Venenbild.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Image of <a href="/wiki/Vein" title="Vein">veins</a> from <a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a>\'s <i>Exercitatio Anatomica de Motu Cordis et Sanguinis in Animalibus</i>. Harvey demonstrated that blood circulated around the body, rather than being created in the liver.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the eighth chapter, Harvey estimated the capacity of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, how much <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> is expelled through each <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">pump</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a>, and the number of times the heart beats in a half an hour. From these estimations, he demonstrated that according to Gaelen\'s theory that blood was continually produced in the liver, the absurdly large figure of 540 pounds of blood would have to be produced every day. Having this simple mathematical proportion at hand \xe2\x80\x93 which would imply a seemingly impossible role for the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> \xe2\x80\x93 Harvey went on to demonstrate how the <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> circulated in a circle by means of countless experiments initially done on <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">serpents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>: tying their <a href="/wiki/Veins" class="mw-redirect" title="Veins">veins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Arteries">arteries</a> in separate periods of time, Harvey noticed the modifications which occurred; indeed, as he tied the <a href="/wiki/Veins" class="mw-redirect" title="Veins">veins</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Heart" title="Heart">heart</a> would become empty, while as he did the same to the arteries, the organ would swell up.\n</p><p>This process was later performed on the human body (in the image on the left): the physician tied a tight ligature onto the upper arm of a person. This would cut off <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> flow from the <a href="/wiki/Arteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Arteries">arteries</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Veins" class="mw-redirect" title="Veins">veins</a>. When this was done, the arm below the <a href="/wiki/Ligature_(medicine)" title="Ligature (medicine)">ligature</a> was cool and pale, while above the ligature it was warm and swollen. The ligature was loosened slightly, which allowed <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Arteries" class="mw-redirect" title="Arteries">arteries</a> to come into the arm, since arteries are deeper in the flesh than the veins. When this was done, the opposite effect was seen in the lower arm. It was now warm and swollen. The <a href="/wiki/Veins" class="mw-redirect" title="Veins">veins</a> were also more visible, since now they were full of <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>.\n</p><p>Various other advances in medical understanding and practice were made. French <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Fauchard" title="Pierre Fauchard">Pierre Fauchard</a> started dentistry science as we know it today, and he has been named "the father of modern dentistry". <a href="/wiki/Surgery" title="Surgery">Surgeon</a> <a href="/wiki/Ambroise_Par%C3%A9" title="Ambroise Par\xc3\xa9">Ambroise Par\xc3\xa9</a> (c.1510\xe2\x80\x931590) was a leader in surgical techniques and <a href="/wiki/Battlefield_medicine" title="Battlefield medicine">battlefield medicine</a>, especially the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Wounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Wounds">wounds</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Herman_Boerhaave" title="Herman Boerhaave">Herman Boerhaave</a> (1668\xe2\x80\x931738) is sometimes referred to as a "father of physiology" due to his exemplary teaching in <a href="/wiki/Leiden" title="Leiden">Leiden</a> and his textbook <i>Institutiones medicae</i> (1708).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sceptical_chymist_1661_Boyle_Title_page_AQ18_(3).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Sceptical_chymist_1661_Boyle_Title_page_AQ18_%283%29.jpg/200px-Sceptical_chymist_1661_Boyle_Title_page_AQ18_%283%29.jpg" width="200" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Sceptical_chymist_1661_Boyle_Title_page_AQ18_%283%29.jpg/300px-Sceptical_chymist_1661_Boyle_Title_page_AQ18_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Sceptical_chymist_1661_Boyle_Title_page_AQ18_%283%29.jpg/400px-Sceptical_chymist_1661_Boyle_Title_page_AQ18_%283%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3744" data-file-height="5616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sceptical_chymist_1661_Boyle_Title_page_AQ18_(3).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sceptical_Chymist" title="The Sceptical Chymist">The Sceptical Chymist</a></i>, a foundational text of chemistry, written by Robert Boyle in 1661</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">Chemistry</a>, and its antecedent <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a>, became an increasingly important aspect of scientific thought in the course of the 16th and 17th centuries. The importance of chemistry is indicated by the range of important scholars who actively engaged in chemical research. Among them were the <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a> <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> the chemical <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> <a href="/wiki/Paracelsus" title="Paracelsus">Paracelsus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Browne" title="Thomas Browne">Thomas Browne</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>. Unlike the mechanical philosophy, the chemical philosophy stressed the active powers of matter, which alchemists frequently expressed in terms of vital or active principles\xe2\x80\x94of spirits operating in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Practical attempts to improve the refining of ores and their extraction to smelt metals were an important source of information for early chemists in the 16th century, among them <a href="/wiki/Georg_Agricola" class="mw-redirect" title="Georg Agricola">Georg Agricola</a> (1494\xe2\x80\x931555), who published his great work <i><a href="/wiki/De_re_metallica" title="De re metallica">De re metallica</a></i> in 1556.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> His work describes the highly developed and complex processes of mining metal ores, metal extraction and metallurgy of the time. His approach removed the mysticism associated with the subject, creating the practical base upon which others could build.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>English chemist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> (1627\xe2\x80\x931691) is considered to have refined the modern scientific method for alchemy and to have separated chemistry further from alchemy.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Although his research clearly has its roots in the <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemical</a> tradition, Boyle is largely regarded today as the first modern chemist, and therefore one of the founders of modern <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, and one of the pioneers of modern experimental <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>. Although Boyle was not the original discover, he is best known for <a href="/wiki/Boyle%27s_law" title="Boyle&#39;s law">Boyle\'s law</a>, which he presented in 1662:<sup id="cite_ref-acottLaw_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acottLaw-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> the law describes the inversely proportional relationship between the absolute <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volume" title="Volume">volume</a> of a gas, if the temperature is kept constant within a <a href="/wiki/Closed_system" title="Closed system">closed system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Boyle is also credited for his landmark publication <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sceptical_Chymist" title="The Sceptical Chymist">The Sceptical Chymist</a></i> in 1661, which is seen as a cornerstone book in the field of chemistry. In the work, Boyle presents his hypothesis that every phenomenon was the result of collisions of particles in motion. Boyle appealed to chemists to experiment and asserted that experiments denied the limiting of chemical elements to only the <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classic four</a>: earth, fire, air, and water. He also pleaded that chemistry should cease to be subservient to <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a> or to alchemy, and rise to the status of a science. Importantly, he advocated a rigorous approach to scientific experiment: he believed all theories must be tested experimentally before being regarded as true. The work contains some of the earliest modern ideas of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reaction</a>, and marks the beginning of the history of modern chemistry.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Physical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<dl><dt>Optics</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Opticks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Opticks.jpg/170px-Opticks.jpg" width="170" height="230" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Opticks.jpg/255px-Opticks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Opticks.jpg/340px-Opticks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="738" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Opticks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Newton\'s</a> <i>Opticks or a treatise of the reflections, refractions, inflections and colours of light</i></div></div></div>\n<p>Important work was done in the field of <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>. <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> published <i>Astronomiae Pars Optica</i> (<i>The Optical Part of Astronomy</i>) in 1604. In it, he described the inverse-square law governing the intensity of light, reflection by flat and curved mirrors, and principles of <a href="/wiki/Pinhole_camera" title="Pinhole camera">pinhole cameras</a>, as well as the astronomical implications of optics such as <a href="/wiki/Parallax" title="Parallax">parallax</a> and the apparent sizes of heavenly bodies.  <i>Astronomiae Pars Optica</i> is generally recognized as the foundation of modern optics (though the <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell&#39;s law">law of refraction</a> is conspicuously absent).<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Willebrord_Snellius" title="Willebrord Snellius">Willebrord Snellius</a> (1580\xe2\x80\x931626) found the mathematical law of <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a>, now known as <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell&#39;s law">Snell\'s law</a>, in 1621. Subsequently <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes">Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes</a> (1596\xe2\x80\x931650) showed, by using geometric construction and the law of refraction (also known as Descartes\' law), that the angular radius of a rainbow is 42\xc2\xb0 (i.e. the angle subtended at the eye by the edge of the rainbow and the rainbow\'s centre is 42\xc2\xb0).<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> He also independently discovered the <a href="/wiki/Specular_reflection" title="Specular reflection">law of reflection</a>, and his essay on optics was the first published mention of this law.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> (1629\xe2\x80\x931695) wrote several works in the area of optics. These included the <i>Opera reliqua</i> (also known as <i>Christiani Hugenii Zuilichemii, dum viveret Zelhemii toparchae, opuscula posthuma</i>) and the <i><a href="/wiki/Trait%C3%A9_de_la_lumi%C3%A8re" class="mw-redirect" title="Trait\xc3\xa9 de la lumi\xc3\xa8re">Trait\xc3\xa9 de la lumi\xc3\xa8re</a></i>.\n</p><p>Isaac Newton investigated the <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">refraction</a> of light, demonstrating that a <a href="/wiki/Dispersive_prism" title="Dispersive prism">prism</a> could decompose <a href="/wiki/White" title="White">white light</a> into a <a href="/wiki/Optical_spectrum" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical spectrum">spectrum</a> of colours, and that a <a href="/wiki/Lens_(optics)" title="Lens (optics)">lens</a> and a second prism could recompose the multicoloured spectrum into white light. He also showed that the coloured light does not change its properties by separating out a coloured beam and shining it on various objects. Newton noted that regardless of whether it was reflected or scattered or transmitted, it stayed the same colour. Thus, he observed that colour is the result of objects interacting with already-coloured light rather than objects generating the colour themselves. This is known as <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Isaac_Newton#Newton&#39;s_theory_of_colour" title="Early life of Isaac Newton">Newton\'s theory of colour</a>. From this work he concluded that any refracting <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> would suffer from the <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">dispersion</a> of light into colours. The interest of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> encouraged him to publish his notes <i>On Colour</i> (later expanded into <i>Opticks</i>). Newton argued that light is composed of particles or <i>corpuscles</i> and were refracted by accelerating toward the denser medium, but he had to associate them with <a href="/wiki/Wave" title="Wave">waves</a> to explain the <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> of light.\n</p><p>In his <i>Hypothesis of Light</i> of 1675, Newton <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/posit" class="extiw" title="wikt:posit">posited</a> the existence of the <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">ether</a> to transmit forces between particles. In 1704, Newton published <i><a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">Opticks</a></i>, in which he expounded his corpuscular theory of light. He considered light to be made up of extremely subtle corpuscles, that ordinary matter was made of grosser corpuscles and speculated that through a kind of alchemical transmutation "Are not gross Bodies and Light convertible into one another, ...and may not Bodies receive much of their Activity from the Particles of Light which enter their Composition?"<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt>Electricity</dt></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Guericke_Sulfur_globe.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Guericke_Sulfur_globe.jpg/220px-Guericke_Sulfur_globe.jpg" width="220" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Guericke_Sulfur_globe.jpg/330px-Guericke_Sulfur_globe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Guericke_Sulfur_globe.jpg/440px-Guericke_Sulfur_globe.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2586" data-file-height="1442" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Guericke_Sulfur_globe.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Guericke" title="Otto von Guericke">Otto von Guericke</a>\'s experiments on <a href="/wiki/Electrostatics" title="Electrostatics">electrostatics</a>, published 1672</div></div></div>\n<p>Dr. <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/De_Magnete" title="De Magnete">De Magnete</a></i>, invented the <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a> word <i>electricus</i> from <i><span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">\xe1\xbc\xa4\xce\xbb\xce\xb5\xce\xba\xcf\x84\xcf\x81\xce\xbf\xce\xbd</span></i> (<i>elektron</i>), the Greek word for "amber". Gilbert undertook a number of careful electrical experiments, in the course of which he discovered that many substances other than amber, such as sulphur, wax, glass, etc.,<sup id="cite_ref-pr_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pr-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> were capable of manifesting electrical properties. Gilbert also discovered that a heated body lost its electricity and that moisture prevented the <a href="/wiki/Electrification" title="Electrification">electrification</a> of all bodies, due to the now well-known fact that moisture impaired the insulation of such bodies.  He also noticed that electrified substances attracted all other substances indiscriminately, whereas a magnet only attracted iron. The many discoveries of this nature earned for Gilbert the title of <i>founder of the electrical science</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-EncyclopediaAmericana_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncyclopediaAmericana-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> By investigating the forces on a light metallic needle, balanced on a point, he extended the list of electric bodies, and found also that many substances, including metals and natural magnets, showed no attractive forces when rubbed. He noticed that dry weather with north or east wind was the most favourable atmospheric condition for exhibiting electric phenomena\xe2\x80\x94an observation liable to misconception until the difference between conductor and insulator was understood.<sup id="cite_ref-Dampier,_W._C._D._92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dampier,_W._C._D.-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Robert Boyle also worked frequently at the new science of electricity, and added several substances to Gilbert\'s list of electrics. He left a detailed account of his researches under the title of <i>Experiments on the Origin of Electricity</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dampier,_W._C._D._92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dampier,_W._C._D.-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Boyle, in 1675, stated that electric attraction and repulsion can act across a vacuum. One of his important discoveries was that electrified bodies in a vacuum would attract light substances, this indicating that the electrical effect did not depend upon the air as a medium. He also added resin to the then known list of electrics.<sup id="cite_ref-pr_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pr-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EncyclopediaAmericana_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncyclopediaAmericana-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This was followed in 1660 by <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Guericke" title="Otto von Guericke">Otto von Guericke</a>, who invented an early <a href="/wiki/Electrostatics" title="Electrostatics">electrostatic</a> generator. By the end of the 17th century, researchers had developed practical means of generating electricity by friction with an <a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_generator" title="Electrostatic generator">electrostatic generator</a>, but the development of electrostatic machines did not begin in earnest until the 18th century, when they became fundamental instruments in the studies about the new science of <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>. The first usage of the word <i>electricity</i> is ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Browne" title="Thomas Browne">Sir Thomas Browne</a> in his 1646 work, <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudodoxia_Epidemica" title="Pseudodoxia Epidemica">Pseudodoxia Epidemica</a></i>. In 1729 <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Gray_(scientist)" title="Stephen Gray (scientist)">Stephen Gray</a> (1666\xe2\x80\x931736) demonstrated that electricity could be "transmitted" through metal filaments.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="New_mechanical_devices">New mechanical devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: New mechanical devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>As an aid to scientific investigation, various tools, measuring aids and calculating devices were developed in this period.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calculating_devices">Calculating devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Calculating devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Napier%27s_Bones.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Napier%27s_Bones.JPG/170px-Napier%27s_Bones.JPG" width="170" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Napier%27s_Bones.JPG/255px-Napier%27s_Bones.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Napier%27s_Bones.JPG/340px-Napier%27s_Bones.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2525" data-file-height="1841" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Napier%27s_Bones.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An ivory set of <a href="/wiki/Napier%27s_Bones" class="mw-redirect" title="Napier&#39;s Bones">Napier\'s Bones</a>, an early calculating device invented by <a href="/wiki/John_Napier" title="John Napier">John Napier</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/John_Napier" title="John Napier">John Napier</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Logarithm" title="Logarithm">logarithms</a> as a powerful mathematical tool. With the help of the prominent mathematician <a href="/wiki/Henry_Briggs_(mathematician)" title="Henry Briggs (mathematician)">Henry Briggs</a> their logarithmic tables embodied a computational advance that made calculations by hand much quicker.<sup id="cite_ref-DNB_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DNB-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Napier%27s_bones" title="Napier&#39;s bones">Napier\'s bones</a> used a set of numbered rods as a multiplication tool using the system of <a href="/wiki/Lattice_multiplication" title="Lattice multiplication">lattice multiplication</a>. The way was opened to later scientific advances, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dynamics_(mechanics)" title="Dynamics (mechanics)">dynamics</a>.\n</p><p>At <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Gunter" title="Edmund Gunter">Edmund Gunter</a> built the first <a href="/wiki/Analog_device" title="Analog device">analog device</a> to aid computation. The \'Gunter\'s scale\' was a large plane scale, engraved with various scales, or lines. Natural lines, such as the line of chords, the line of <a href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">sines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tangent_(trigonometric_function)" class="mw-redirect" title="Tangent (trigonometric function)">tangents</a> are placed on one side of the scale and the corresponding artificial or logarithmic ones were on the other side. This calculating aid was a predecessor of the <a href="/wiki/Slide_rule#History" title="Slide rule">slide rule</a>. It was <a href="/wiki/William_Oughtred" title="William Oughtred">William Oughtred</a> (1575\xe2\x80\x931660) who first used two such scales sliding by one another to perform direct <a href="/wiki/Multiplication" title="Multiplication">multiplication</a> and <a href="/wiki/Division_(mathematics)" title="Division (mathematics)">division</a>, and thus is credited as the inventor of the <a href="/wiki/Slide_rule" title="Slide rule">slide rule</a> in 1622.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal</a> (1623\xe2\x80\x931662) invented the <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_calculator#The_17th_century" title="Mechanical calculator">mechanical calculator</a> in 1642.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The introduction of his <a href="/wiki/Pascaline" class="mw-redirect" title="Pascaline">Pascaline</a> in 1645 launched the development of mechanical calculators first in Europe and then all over the world.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz" class="mw-redirect" title="Gottfried Leibniz">Gottfried Leibniz</a> (1646\xe2\x80\x931716), building on Pascal\'s work, became one of the most prolific inventors in the field of mechanical calculators; he was the first to describe a <a href="/wiki/Pinwheel_calculator" title="Pinwheel calculator">pinwheel calculator</a>, in 1685,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> and invented the <a href="/wiki/Leibniz_wheel" title="Leibniz wheel">Leibniz wheel</a>, used in the <a href="/wiki/Arithmometer" title="Arithmometer">arithmometer</a>, the first mass-produced mechanical calculator. He also refined the binary number system, foundation of virtually all modern computer architectures.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Hadley" title="John Hadley">John Hadley</a> (1682\xe2\x80\x931744) was the inventor of the <a href="/wiki/Octant_(instrument)" title="Octant (instrument)">octant</a>, the precursor to the <a href="/wiki/Sextant" title="Sextant">sextant</a> (invented by <a href="/wiki/John_Bird_(astronomer)" title="John Bird (astronomer)">John Bird)</a>, which greatly improved the science of <a href="/wiki/Navigation" title="Navigation">navigation</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_machines">Industrial machines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Industrial machines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Savery-engine.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Savery-engine.jpg/170px-Savery-engine.jpg" width="170" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Savery-engine.jpg/255px-Savery-engine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Savery-engine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="318" data-file-height="426" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Savery-engine.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1698 <i><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Savery" title="Thomas Savery">Savery Engine</a></i> was the first successful <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Denis_Papin" title="Denis Papin">Denis Papin</a> (1647\xe2\x80\x931712) was best known for his pioneering invention of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_digester" title="Steam digester">steam digester</a>, the forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The first working steam engine was patented in 1698 by the inventor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Savery" title="Thomas Savery">Thomas Savery</a>, as a "...new invention for raising of water and occasioning motion to all sorts of mill work by the impellent force of fire, which will be of great use and advantage for drayning mines, serveing townes with water, and for the working of all sorts of mills where they have not the benefitt of water nor constant windes."&#32;&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>&#93;<sup id="cite_ref-jenkins_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jenkins-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The invention was demonstrated to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> on 14 June 1699 and the machine was described by Savery in his book <i>The Miner\'s Friend; or, An Engine to Raise Water by Fire</i> (1702),<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> in which he claimed that it could pump water out of <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mines</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Newcomen" title="Thomas Newcomen">Thomas Newcomen</a> (1664\xe2\x80\x931729) perfected the practical steam engine for pumping water, the <a href="/wiki/Newcomen_steam_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Newcomen steam engine">Newcomen steam engine</a>. Consequently, Thomas Newcomen can be regarded as a forefather of the Industrial Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Darby_I" title="Abraham Darby I">Abraham Darby I</a> (1678\xe2\x80\x931717) was the first, and most famous, of three generations of the Darby family who played an important role in the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. He developed a method of producing high-grade iron in a <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a> fueled by <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>. This was a major step forward in the production of iron as a raw material for the Industrial Revolution.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Telescopes">Telescopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Telescopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">Refracting telescopes</a> first appeared in the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> in 1608, apparently the product of spectacle makers experimenting with lenses. The inventor is unknown but <a href="/wiki/Hans_Lippershey" title="Hans Lippershey">Hans Lippershey</a> applied for the first patent, followed by <a href="/wiki/Jacob_Metius" title="Jacob Metius">Jacob Metius</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alkmaar" title="Alkmaar">Alkmaar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> was one of the first scientists to use this new tool for his astronomical observations in 1609.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Reflecting_telescope" title="Reflecting telescope">reflecting telescope</a> was described by <a href="/wiki/James_Gregory_(astronomer_and_mathematician)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Gregory (astronomer and mathematician)">James Gregory</a> in his book <i>Optica Promota</i> (1663). He argued that a mirror shaped like the part of a <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic section</a>, would correct the <a href="/wiki/Spherical_aberration" title="Spherical aberration">spherical aberration</a> that flawed the accuracy of refracting telescopes. His design, the "<a href="/wiki/Gregorian_telescope" title="Gregorian telescope">Gregorian telescope</a>", however, remained un-built.\n</p><p>In 1666, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> argued that the faults of the refracting telescope were fundamental because the lens refracted light of different colors differently. He concluded that light could not be refracted through a lens without causing <a href="/wiki/Chromatic_aberration" title="Chromatic aberration">chromatic aberrations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> From these experiments Newton concluded that no improvement could be made in the refracting telescope.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> However, he was able to demonstrate that the angle of reflection remained the same for all colors, so he decided to build a <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_reflector" title="Newton&#39;s reflector">reflecting telescope</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> It was completed in 1668 and is the earliest known functional reflecting telescope.<sup id="cite_ref-mymathdone.com_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mymathdone.com-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>50 years later, <a href="/wiki/John_Hadley" title="John Hadley">John Hadley</a> developed ways to make precision aspheric and <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_reflector" title="Parabolic reflector">parabolic</a> <a href="/wiki/Objective_(optics)" title="Objective (optics)">objective</a> mirrors for <a href="/wiki/Reflecting_telescope" title="Reflecting telescope">reflecting telescopes</a>, building the first parabolic <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_telescope" title="Newtonian telescope">Newtonian telescope</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_telescope" title="Gregorian telescope">Gregorian telescope</a> with accurately shaped mirrors.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> These were successfully demonstrated to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_devices">Other devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boyle_air_pump.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Boyle_air_pump.jpg/170px-Boyle_air_pump.jpg" width="170" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Boyle_air_pump.jpg/255px-Boyle_air_pump.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Boyle_air_pump.jpg/340px-Boyle_air_pump.jpg 2x" data-file-width="706" data-file-height="1116" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boyle_air_pump.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Air_pump" title="Air pump">Air pump</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a>. Many new instruments were devised in this period, which greatly aided in the expansion of scientific knowledge.</div></div></div>\n<p>The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_pump" title="Vacuum pump">vacuum pump</a> paved the way for the experiments of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> into the nature of <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a>. The first such device was made by <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_Guericke" title="Otto von Guericke">Otto von Guericke</a> in 1654. It consisted of a piston and an <a href="/wiki/Air_gun#History" title="Air gun">air gun cylinder</a> with flaps that could suck the air from any vessel that it was connected to. In 1657, he pumped the air out of two conjoined hemispheres and demonstrated that a team of sixteen horses were incapable of pulling it apart.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The air pump construction was greatly improved by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> in 1658.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">Evangelista Torricelli</a> (1607\xe2\x80\x931647) was best known for his invention of the mercury <a href="/wiki/Barometer" title="Barometer">barometer</a>. The motivation for the invention was to improve on the suction pumps that were used to raise water out of the <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mines</a>. Torricelli constructed a sealed tube filled with mercury, set vertically into a basin of the same substance. The column of mercury fell downwards, leaving a Torricellian vacuum above.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Timbs_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Timbs-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Materials.2C_construction.2C_and_aesthetics"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Materials,_construction,_and_aesthetics">Materials, construction, and aesthetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Materials, construction, and aesthetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Surviving instruments from this period,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> tend to be made of durable metals such as brass, gold, or steel, although examples such as telescopes<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> made of wood, pasteboard, or with leather components exist.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Those instruments that exist in collections today tend to be robust examples, made by skilled craftspeople for and at the expense of wealthy patrons.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> These may have been commissioned as displays of wealth. In addition, the instruments preserved in collections may not have received heavy use in scientific work; instruments that had visibly received heavy use were typically destroyed, deemed unfit for display, or excluded from collections altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> It is also postulated that the scientific instruments preserved in many collections were chosen because they were more appealing to collectors, by virtue of being more ornate, more portable, or made with higher-grade materials.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Intact air pumps are particularly rare.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The pump at right included a glass sphere to permit demonstrations inside the vacuum chamber, a common use. The base was wooden, and the cylindrical pump was brass.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Other vacuum chambers that survived were made of brass hemispheres.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Instrument makers of the late seventeenth and early eighteenth century were commissioned by organizations seeking help with navigation, surveying, warfare, and astronomical observation.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The increase in uses for such instruments, and their widespread use in global exploration and conflict, created a need for new methods of manufacture and repair, which would be met by the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_developments">Scientific developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Scientific developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>People and key ideas that emerged from the 16th and 17th centuries:\n</p>\n<ul><li>First printed edition of <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid&#39;s Elements">Euclid\'s Elements</a> in 1482.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> (1473\xe2\x80\x931543) published <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Revolutions_of_the_Heavenly_Spheres" class="mw-redirect" title="On the Revolutions of the Heavenly Spheres">On the Revolutions of the Heavenly Spheres</a></i> in 1543, which advanced the <a href="/wiki/Heliocentric_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Heliocentric theory">heliocentric theory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Andreas_Vesalius" title="Andreas Vesalius">Andreas Vesalius</a> (1514\xe2\x80\x931564) published <i><a href="/wiki/De_Humani_Corporis_Fabrica" class="mw-redirect" title="De Humani Corporis Fabrica">De Humani Corporis Fabrica</a></i> (<i>On the Structure of the Human Body</i>) (1543), which discredited <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>\'s views. He found that the circulation of blood resolved from pumping of the heart. He also assembled the first human skeleton from cutting open cadavers.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Franciscus_Vieta" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscus Vieta">Franciscus Vieta</a> (1540\xe2\x80\x931603) published <i><a href="/wiki/New_algebra" class="mw-redirect" title="New algebra">In Artem Analycitem Isagoge</a></i> (1591), which gave the first symbolic notation of parameters in literal algebra.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a> (1544\xe2\x80\x931603) published <i><a href="/wiki/De_Magnete" title="De Magnete">On the Magnet and Magnetic Bodies, and on the Great Magnet the Earth</a></i> in 1600, which laid the foundations of a theory of <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> (1546\xe2\x80\x931601) made extensive and more accurate naked eye observations of the planets in the late 16th century. These became the basic data for Kepler\'s studies.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon_(philosopher)" class="mw-redirect" title="Francis Bacon (philosopher)">Sir Francis Bacon</a> (1561\xe2\x80\x931626) published <i><a href="/wiki/Novum_Organum" title="Novum Organum">Novum Organum</a></i> in 1620, which outlined a new system of <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a> based on the process of <a href="/wiki/Reduction_(philosophy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reduction (philosophy)">reduction</a>, which he offered as an improvement over <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a> process of <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogism</a>. This contributed to the development of what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> (1564\xe2\x80\x931642) improved the <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>, with which he made several important astronomical observations, including the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">four largest moons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> (1610), the phases of <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> (1610 - proving Copernicus correct), the rings of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> (1610), and made detailed observations of <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a>. He developed the laws for falling bodies based on pioneering quantitative experiments which he analyzed mathematically.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> (1571\xe2\x80\x931630) published the first two of his three <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler&#39;s laws of planetary motion">laws of planetary motion</a> in 1609.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/William_Harvey" title="William Harvey">William Harvey</a> (1578\xe2\x80\x931657) demonstrated that blood circulates, using dissections and other experimental techniques.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes">Ren\xc3\xa9 Descartes</a> (1596\xe2\x80\x931650) published his <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Method" title="Discourse on the Method">Discourse on the Method</a></i> in 1637, which helped to establish the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a> (1632\xe2\x80\x931723) constructed powerful single lens microscopes and made extensive observations that he published around 1660, opening up the micro-world of biology.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> (1629\xe2\x80\x931695) published major studies of mechanics (he was the first one to correctly formulate laws concerning centrifugal force and discovered the theory of the pendulum) and optics (being one of the most influential proponents of the wave theory of light).</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> (1643\xe2\x80\x931727) built upon the work of Kepler, Galileo and Huygens. He showed that an inverse square law for gravity explained the elliptical orbits of the planets, and advanced the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation">law of universal gravitation</a>. His development of <a href="/wiki/Infinitesimal_calculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Infinitesimal calculus">infinitesimal calculus</a> (along with Leibniz) opened up new applications of the methods of mathematics to science. Newton taught that scientific theory should be coupled with rigorous experimentation, which became the keystone of modern science.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Continuity_thesis" title="Continuity thesis">Continuity thesis</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ricci_Guangqi_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ricci_Guangqi_2.jpg/170px-Ricci_Guangqi_2.jpg" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ricci_Guangqi_2.jpg/255px-Ricci_Guangqi_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Ricci_Guangqi_2.jpg/340px-Ricci_Guangqi_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3338" data-file-height="4988" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ricci_Guangqi_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">Matteo Ricci</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Xu_Guangqi" title="Xu Guangqi">Xu Guangqi</a> (right) in <a href="/wiki/Athanasius_Kircher" title="Athanasius Kircher">Athanasius Kircher</a>, <i>La Chine ... Illustr\xc3\xa9e</i>, Amsterdam, 1670.</div></div></div>\n<p>The idea that modern science took place as a kind of revolution has been debated among historians. A weakness of the idea of scientific revolution is the lack of a systematic approach to the question of knowledge in the period comprehended between the 14th and 17th centuries, leading to misunderstandings on the value and role of modern authors. From this standpoint, the continuity thesis is the hypothesis that there was no radical discontinuity between the intellectual development of the Middle Ages and the developments in the Renaissance and early modern period and has been deeply and widely documented by the works of scholars like Pierre Duhem, John Hermann Randall, Alistair Crombie and William A. Wallace, who proved the preexistence of a wide range of ideas used by the followers of the Scientific Revolution thesis to substantiate their claims. Thus, the idea of a scientific revolution following the Renaissance is\xe2\x80\x94according to the continuity thesis\xe2\x80\x94a myth. Some continuity theorists point to earlier intellectual revolutions occurring in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, usually referring to either a European <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_of_the_12th_century" title="Renaissance of the 12th century">Renaissance of the 12th century</a><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> or a medieval <a href="/wiki/Science_in_medieval_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Science in medieval Islam">Muslim scientific revolution</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> as a sign of continuity.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Another contrary view has been recently proposed by Arun Bala in his <a href="/wiki/Dialogue" title="Dialogue">dialogical</a> history of the birth of modern science. Bala proposes that the changes involved in the Scientific Revolution\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">mathematical realist</a> turn, the mechanical <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Atomism" title="Atomism">atomism</a>, the central role assigned to the Sun in <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">Copernican heliocentrism</a>\xe2\x80\x94have to be seen as rooted in <a href="/wiki/Multiculturalism" title="Multiculturalism">multicultural</a> influences on Europe.  He sees specific influences in <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Haytham" title="Ibn al-Haytham">Alhazen</a>\'s physical optical theory, <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_China" title="History of science and technology in China">Chinese mechanical technologies</a> leading to the perception of the world as a <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">machine</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hindu-Arabic_numeral_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu-Arabic numeral system">Hindu-Arabic numeral system</a>, which carried implicitly a new mode of <a href="/wiki/Atomism#Indian_atomism" title="Atomism">mathematical atomic thinking</a>, and the heliocentrism rooted in ancient Egyptian religious ideas associated with <a href="/wiki/Hermeticism" title="Hermeticism">Hermeticism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bala argues that by ignoring such multicultural impacts we have been led to a <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">Eurocentric</a> conception of the Scientific Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>  However, he clearly states: "The makers of the revolution \xe2\x80\x93 Copernicus, Kepler, Galileo, Descartes, Newton, and many others \xe2\x80\x93 had to selectively appropriate relevant ideas, transform them, and create new auxiliary concepts in order to complete their task... In the ultimate analysis, even if the revolution was rooted upon a multicultural base it is the accomplishment of Europeans in Europe."<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>  Critics note that lacking documentary evidence of transmission of specific scientific ideas, Bala\'s model will remain "a working hypothesis, not a conclusion".<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A third approach takes the term "Renaissance" literally as a "rebirth". A closer study of <a href="/wiki/Greek_philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek philosophy">Greek philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_mathematics" title="Greek mathematics">Greek mathematics</a> demonstrates that nearly all of the so-called revolutionary results of the so-called scientific revolution were in actuality restatements of ideas that were in many cases older than those of <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and in nearly all cases at least as old as <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>. Aristotle even explicitly argues against some of the ideas that were espoused during the Scientific Revolution, such as <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentrism</a>. The basic ideas of the scientific method were well known to Archimedes and his contemporaries, as demonstrated in the well-known discovery of <a href="/wiki/Buoyancy" title="Buoyancy">buoyancy</a>. Atomism was first thought of by <a href="/wiki/Leucippus" title="Leucippus">Leucippus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a>. Lucio Russo claims that science as a unique approach to objective knowledge was born in the Hellenistic period (c. 300 B.C), but was extinguished with the advent of the Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> This approach to the Scientific Revolution reduces it to a period of relearning classical ideas that is very much an extension of the Renaissance. This view does not deny that a change occurred but argues that it was a reassertion of previous knowledge (a renaissance) and not the creation of new knowledge. It cites statements from Newton, Copernicus and others in favour of the <a href="/wiki/Pythagoreanism" title="Pythagoreanism">Pythagorean</a> worldview as evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In more recent analysis of the Scientific Revolution during this period, there has been criticism of not only the Eurocentric ideologies spread, but also of the dominance of male scientists of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Science as we know it today, and the original theories that we base modern science on, was built by males, regardless of the input women might have made. The incorporation of women\'s work in the sciences during this time tends to be obscured. Scholars have tried to look into the participation of women in the 17th century in science, and even with sciences as simple as domestic knowledge women were making advances.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> With the limited history provided from texts of the period we are not completely aware if women were helping these scientists develop the ideas they did. Another idea to consider is the way this period influenced even the women scientists of the periods following it. Annie Jump Cannon was an astronomer who benefitted from the laws and theories developed from this period; she made several advances in the century following the Scientific Revolution. It was an important period for the future of science, including the incorporation of women into fields using the developments made.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chemical_revolution" title="Chemical revolution">Chemical revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Information_revolution" title="Information revolution">Information revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/The_Structure_of_Scientific_Revolutions" title="The Structure of Scientific Revolutions">The Structure of Scientific Revolutions</a> (Book)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Scientific_Revolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-Galileo-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Galileo_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Galileo_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Galilei, Galileo (1974) <i>Two New Sciences</i>, trans. <a href="/wiki/Stillman_Drake" title="Stillman Drake">Stillman Drake</a>, (Madison: Univ. of Wisconsin Pr. pp. 217, 225, 296\xe2\x80\x937.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Moody-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Moody_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Moody_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Moody, Ernest A. (1951). "Galileo and Avempace: The Dynamics of the Leaning Tower Experiment (I)". <i>Journal of the History of Ideas</i>. <b>12</b> (2): 163\xe2\x80\x93193. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.2307%2F2707514">10.2307/2707514</a>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR" title="JSTOR">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a (1966). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160304064640/http://ls.poly.edu/~jbain/mms/texts/66McGuire(Pipes).pdf">"Newton and the \'Pipes of Pan<span class="cs1-kern-right">\'</span>"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Notes and Records of the Royal Society  <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160304064640/http://ls.poly.edu/~jbain/mms/texts/66McGuire(Pipes).pdf">https://web.archive.org/web/20160304064640/http://ls.poly.edu/~jbain/mms/texts/66McGuire(Pipes).pdf</a></i>. <b>21</b> (2): 108. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1098%2Frsnr.1966.0014">10.1098/rsnr.1966.0014</a>. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ls.poly.edu/~jbain/mms/texts/66McGuire%28Pipes%29.pdf">the original</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> on 4 March 2016.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Notes+and+Records+of+the+Royal+Society++https%3A%2F%2Fweb.archive.org%2Fweb%2F20160304064640%2Fhttp%3A%2F%2Fls.poly.edu%2F~jbain%2Fmms%2Ftexts%2F66McGuire%28Pipes%29.pdf&amp;rft.atitle=Newton+and+the+%27Pipes+of+Pan%27&amp;rft.volume=21&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.pages=108&amp;rft.date=1966&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1098%2Frsnr.1966.0014&amp;rft.aulast=McGuire&amp;rft.aufirst=J.+E.&amp;rft.au=Rattansi%2C+P.+M.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fls.poly.edu%2F~jbain%2Fmms%2Ftexts%2F66McGuire%2528Pipes%2529.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AScientific+Revolution" class="Z3988"></span> <span class="cs1-visible-error error citation-comment">External link in <code class="cs1-code">&#124;journal=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#param_has_ext_link" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-Unpublished_Scientific_Papers_of_Isaac_Newton-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Unpublished_Scientific_Papers_of_Isaac_Newton_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Newton, Isaac (1962).  Hall, A.R.; Hall, M.B., eds. <i>Unpublished Scientific Papers of Isaac Newton</i>. Cambridge University Press. pp.&#160;310\xe2\x80\x9311. <q>All those ancients knew the first law [of motion] who attributed to atoms in an infinite vacuum a motion which was rectilinear, extremely swift and perpetual because of the lack of resistance... Aristotle was of the same mind, since he expresses his opinion thus...[in <i>Physics</i> 4.8.215a19-22], speaking of motion in the void [in which bodies have no gravity and] where there is no impediment he writes: \'Why a body once moved should come to rest anywhere no one can say. For why should it rest here rather than there&#160;? Hence either it will not be moved, or it must be moved indefinitely, unless something stronger impedes it.\'</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Unpublished+Scientific+Papers+of+Isaac+Newton&amp;rft.pages=310-11&amp;rft.pub=Cambridge+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1962&amp;rft.aulast=Newton&amp;rft.aufirst=Isaac&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AScientific+Revolution" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Sorabji, R. (2005). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=0QpQw8JDgQcC&amp;lpg=PA348&amp;pg=PA348"><i>The Philosophy of the Commentators, 200\xe2\x80\x93600 AD: Physics</i></a>. G \xe2\x80\x93 Reference, Information and Interdisciplinary Subjects Series. Cornell University Press. p.&#160;348. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8014-8988-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8014-8988-4">978-0-8014-8988-4</a>. <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Control_Number" title="Library of Congress Control Number">LCCN</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//lccn.loc.gov/2004063547">2004063547</a>. <q>An impetus is an inner force impressed into a moving body from without. It thus contrasts with purely external forces like the action of air on projectiles in Aristotle, and with purely internal forces like the nature of the elements in Aristotle and his followers.\xe2\x80\xa6 Impetus theories also contrast with theories of inertia which replaced them in the seventeenth to eighteenth centuries.\xe2\x80\xa6 Such inertial ideas are merely sporadic in Antiquity and not consciously attended to as a separate option. Aristotle, for example, argues in <i>Phys.</i> 4.8 that in a vacuum a moving body would never stop, but the possible implications for inertia are not discussed.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Philosophy+of+the+Commentators%2C+200%E2%80%93600+AD%3A+Physics&amp;rft.series=G+%E2%80%93+Reference%2C+Information+and+Interdisciplinary+Subjects+Series&amp;rft.pages=348&amp;rft.pub=Cornell+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft_id=info%3Alccn%2F2004063547&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8014-8988-4&amp;rft.aulast=Sorabji&amp;rft.aufirst=R.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D0QpQw8JDgQcC%26lpg%3DPA348%26pg%3DPA348&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AScientific+Revolution" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Heath, Thomas L.
